Here are 5 important and unique points extracted from the article:

  1. Large-scale protests: Over 90,000 people participated in labor union rallies across 15 regions in South Korea, including Seoul, to demand better labor policies and higher minimum wages.

  2. Protest actions: Demonstrators blocked roads, occupied sidewalks, and even attempted to "arrest" a dummy of President Yoon Suk-yeol, symbolizing their dissatisfaction with the government's labor policies.

  3. Demands: Protesting labor unions, such as the Korean Confederation of Trade Unions (KCTU) and the Federation of Korean Trade Unions (FKTU), are demanding better labor conditions, higher minimum wages, and the withdrawal of the government's "Labor Basic Act" reform.

  4. Impact on daily life: The massive protests caused some inconvenience to commuters and residents in the surrounding areas, with some roads and sidewalks blocked off.

  5. Preparations for next steps: After the protests, labor unions are expected to hold more activities and negotiations with the government to push for their demands, considering the protests as a "beginning" of their labor movement. Here are the 5 key and unique points from the article:

  1. Japanese research institute analysis: According to Meiji Yasuda Research Institute, if the yen-dollar exchange rate reaches 170 yen, the relative purchasing power of wages will decrease by 13.5%, and the growth rate of real wages will continue to decline.
  2. Impact on food prices: The research institute predicts that if the exchange rate reaches 160 yen, food prices will rise by 8.7%. This means that the prices of over 417 food items will increase, with an average increase rate of 31%.
  3. Delays in real wage growth: The institute also forecasts that the timing of real wage growth will be delayed as the exchange rate rises, making it difficult for Japanese workers to increase their purchasing power.
  4. Supply chain effects: The article mentions that the recent sharp decline in the yen may lead to a chain reaction of price increases in the fall, as imported goods become more expensive.

  5. Japan's economic downturn: The article also notes that Japan's real gross domestic product (GDP) is expected to contract in the first quarter of this year, marking the first contraction in two years, according to a survey of 10 private-sector economists. Here are 5 important and unique points from the article:

  1. Brazil nuts boost selenium levels: Brazil nuts are rich in selenium, an essential mineral that plays a crucial role in immune system function and thyroid health.

  2. Selenium lowers TPO antibodies: Selenium has been shown to lower TPO (thyroid peroxidase) antibodies, which are associated with Hashimoto's thyroiditis and can lead to hypothyroidism.

  3. Selenium reduces oxidative stress and inflammation: Selenium has antioxidant properties that help reduce oxidative stress and inflammation in the body, which can mitigate the risk of chronic diseases such as cancer, diabetes, and heart disease.

  4. Brazil nuts support brain health: Brazil nuts have been shown to support brain health and cognitive function, potentially reducing the risk of neurodegenerative diseases such as Alzheimer's and Parkinson's.

  5. Brazil nuts may reduce risk of thyroid cancer: High levels of selenium have been linked to a reduced risk of thyroid cancer, making Brazil nuts a potentially useful supplement for thyroid health.
